      C OMBINATORIAL R EINFORCEMENT L EARNING WITH
002   P REFERENCE F EEDBACK

006    Paper under double-blind review

009                                                     A BSTRACT
010
011             In this paper, we consider combinatorial reinforcement learning with preference
012             feedback, where a learning agent sequentially offers an action—an assortment of
013             multiple items—to a user, whose preference feedback follows a multinomial logit
014             (MNL) model. This framework allows us to model real-world scenarios, particu-
015             larly those involving long-term user engagement, such as in recommender systems
016             and online advertising. However, this framework faces two main challenges: 1) the
017             unknown value of each item, unlike traditional MNL bandits (which only account
018             for single-step preference feedback), and (2) the difficulty of ensuring optimism
019
                with tractable assortment selection in the combinatorial action space. In this paper,
                we assume a contextual MNL preference model, where mean utilities are linear,
020
                and the value of each item is approximated using general function approximation.
021
                We propose an algorithm, MNL-VQL, that addresses these challenges, making it
022             both computationally and statistically efficient. As a special case, for linear MDPs
023             (with the MNL preference feedback), we establish the first regret lower bound
024             in this framework and show that MNL-VQL achieves near-optimal regret. To the
025             best of our knowledge, this is the first work to provide statistical guarantees in
026             combinatorial RL with preference feedback.
027
028
029   1    I NTRODUCTION
030
031   We first formally state the concept of Combinatorial Reinforcement Learning (RL), which we refer to
032   as a class of RL problems where the action space is combinatorial, meaning that the agent selects a
033   combination or subset of base actions from a set of possible base actions. Although some previous
034   studies have addressed problems within this setting—particularly in deep RL (Sunehag et al., 2015;
035   He et al., 2016; Swaminathan et al., 2017; Metz et al., 2017; Ryu et al., 2019; Ie et al., 2019; Delarue
036   et al., 2020; McInerney et al., 2020; Vlassis et al., 2021; Chaudhari et al., 2024), with less emphasis
037   on theoretical RL—to the best of our knowledge, it appears that no prior work has formally and
038
      theoretically defined the concept of combinatorial RL.1 This framework is especially relevant for real-
      world applications such as recommender systems and online advertising, where multiple items (base
039
      actions) must be selected simultaneously, such as a set of products to recommend or advertisements
040
      to display. The challenge in combinatorial RL lies in the exponentially large action space and the
041   need to efficiently optimize the agent’s action selection while balancing exploration and exploitation
042   (a challenge even for single action selection), while considering the long-term effects of these actions.
043
044
      One of the most widely encountered settings in combinatorial RL is preference feedback over
      combinatorial actions, commonly seen in streaming services, online retail, and similar platforms.
045
      Despite the broad applicability of this setting, theoretical studies have predominantly focused on
046
      the multinomial logit (MNL) bandit model (Rusmevichientong et al., 2010; Sauré & Zeevi, 2013;
047   Agrawal et al., 2017; 2019; Oh & Iyengar, 2019; 2021; Perivier & Goyal, 2022; Agrawal et al., 2023;
048   Zhang & Sugiyama, 2024; Lee & Oh, 2024). The MNL bandit framework focuses on assortment (a
049   set of items) selection by selecting subsets of items and receiving feedback on chosen items, modeled
050   by the MNL model (McFadden, 1977). However, these studies take a myopic approach, optimizing
051   for immediate rewards without considering the long-term impact on user behavior.
052
        1
053       Surprisingly, this is in contrast to the rich theoretical literature in combinatorial bandits (Chen et al., 2013;
      Kveton et al., 2015b;a; Combes et al., 2015), which extend the multi-armed bandit problem.


                                                               1
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


054
      While MNL bandits have been widely studied, the myopic approach is limiting in many real-
055   world scenarios. For example in recommender systems, incorporating the long-term impact of
056   recommendations opens the door to balancing short-term engagement with long-term user satisfaction.
057   For instance, recommending junk product or content might lead to high immediate reward but it can
058   decrease user satisfaction over time due to fatigue. This trade-off between immediate and long-term
059   outcomes is not captured by traditional MNL bandit approaches. See Appendix A for a more details.
060
      On the empirical side, several studies have explored long-term user engagement in recommendation
061   systems, particularly using deep RL (Swaminathan et al., 2017; Ie et al., 2019; McInerney et al., 2020;
062   Vlassis et al., 2021; Chaudhari et al., 2024). However, there is a significant gap in the theoretical
063   understanding of combinatorial RL with preference feedback, particularly within the RL framework.
064   To the best of our knowledge, no theoretical work has yet explored this important problem setting.
065
      In this paper, we aim to address this gap by rigorously studying the problem of combinatorial RL with
066
      preference feedback. Our goal is to develop a provably efficient algorithm that maximizes long-term
067   user engagement by incorporating state transitions (e.g., historical behavior) in decision-making.
068   The key challenges in this framework are: (1) the unknown long-term value of each item due to the
069   stochastic nature of rewards and transitions, (2) the difficulty of selecting an assortment that ensures
070   optimism while considering tractable assortment optimization in the combinatorial action space.
071
      To tackle theses challenges, we first decompose the Q-function of an assortment into two components:
072
      the preference model and the item values, inspired by Ie et al. (2019). Since the long-term value of
073   each item is unknown (unlike in MNL bandits), we compute optimistic item values and select an
074   assortment based on those values. To ensure optimism (Lemma D.9), we propose a novel method to
075   estimate the optimistic preference model by carefully alternating between optimistic and pessimistic
076   utilities (for the preference model) based on the optimistic item values (Equation 7 and 8). This
077   method requires a more sophisticated proof analysis to address both cases (Lemma D.2, D.5, and D.7).
078   Finally, once the optimistic item values and optimistic (or pessimistic) utilities are established, we
079   avoid the need for naive combinatorial enumeration when selecting the assortment (Equation 9) by
080   reformulating the optimization problem as a linear programming (LP), inspired by Davis et al. (2013).
081   In this paper, We consider the contextual MNL preference model with linear mean utilities (Agrawal
082   & Goyal, 2013; Cheung & Simchi-Levi, 2017; Agrawal et al., 2019; Oh & Iyengar, 2019; 2021;
083   Amani & Thrampoulidis, 2021; Perivier & Goyal, 2022; Agrawal et al., 2023; Zhang & Sugiyama,
084   2024; Lee & Oh, 2024) and use general function approximation to estimate item values (Jiang et al.,
085   2017; Wang et al., 2020; Jin et al., 2021; Du et al., 2021; Foster et al., 2021; Agarwal et al., 2023;
086   Zhao et al., 2023). Inspired by Agarwal et al. (2023), we use function approximation in the Q-type
087
      setting (Jin et al., 2021), employing point-wise optimism for estimating item values. The algorithm
      then constructs optimistic choice probabilities based on optimistic or pessimistic MNL utilities to
088
      ensure sufficient exploration and guarantee optimism. Our main contributions are summarized as:
089
090          • We propose `a computationally     efficient algorithm,
091
                                   ?       ?                      ˘ MNL-VQL, that achieves a regret upper
               bound of Õ dH K ` dimpFqKH log |F| , where H is the horizon length, K is the
092            total number of episodes, d is the feature dimension of the MNL preference model, and
093            dimpFq is the generalized Eluder dimension (see Definition 3) of the function class F, under
094            the parameterized contextual MNL preference model and general function approximation
095            for item values (Theorem 1). To the best of our knowledge, this is the first theoretical regret
096            guarantee in combinatorial RL with preference feedback.
097                                                                                                    ?
             • For? the special case of linear MDPs, MNL-VQL obtains a regret upper bound of ÕpdH K `
098
               dlin HKq, where dlin is the feature dimension of?the linear MDPs? (Theorem 2). Further-
099
               more, we establish a regret lower bound of Ωpd HK ` dlin HKq, demonstrating the
100
               near-optimality of our algorithm for linear MDPs (Theorem 3). To the best of our knowledge,
101            this is the first regret lower bound in linear MDPs with preference feedback.
102
103
104   2    R ELATED W ORK
105
106   MNL bandits. The MNL bandits were initially studied in Rusmevichientong et al. (2010), followed
107   by a line of improvements (Filippi et al., 2010; Rusmevichientong et al., 2010; Agrawal et al., 2017;
      Oh & Iyengar, 2019; Faury et al., 2020; Abeille et al., 2021; Faury et al., 2022; Oh & Iyengar, 2021;


                                                         2
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


108
      Perivier & Goyal, 2022; Agrawal et al., 2023; Lee & Oh, 2024). In MNL bandits, the goal is to
109   offer an assortment that maximizes the expected rewards, which are adaptively learned based on user
110   preference feedback from the offered assortment. However, there are no state transitions, and it is
111   assumed that the value of each item is known, with the value of the outside option fixed at zero. In
112   contrast, our study extends this by not only estimating the MNL model but also the item values.
113
      Combinatorial RL with preference feedback. Recently, several studies have demonstrated the
114
      empirical success of combinatorial RL with preference feedback (Swaminathan et al., 2017; Ie et al.,
115   2019; McInerney et al., 2020; Vlassis et al., 2021; Chaudhari et al., 2024), where a set of items is
116   offered to a user, and (relative) choice feedback along with a reward is received, leading to a transition
117   to the next state. However, theoretical results quantifying the benefits of such methods are still few
118   and far between. A closely related work is cascading RL (Du et al., 2024), which also involves
119   selecting a set of items. However, in cascading RL, items are offered to the user one by one, and
120   the user decides only whether to choose the currently offered item. As a result, this framework does
121   not capture relative preference feedback across multiple items. Furthermore, in cascading RL, the
122   probability of choosing each item is independent of the others, which is not the case in our framework.
123   Another related line of work is preference-based RL (PbRL) (Akrour et al., 2012; Wirth et al., 2017;
124   Christiano et al., 2017; Ouyang et al., 2022; Saha et al., 2023; Zhu et al., 2023; Zhan et al., 2023),
125   where the policy is optimized based on relative, rather than absolute, preference feedback. However,
126   our framework differs from PbRL in that our goal is not to offer just a single item, but to offer multiple
127   items—a combinatorial (base) action—at each timestep.
128   Provably efficient RL with nonlinear function approximation. RL under nonlinear function
129   approximation has gained attention (Jiang et al., 2017; Wang et al., 2020; Jin et al., 2021; Du et al.,
130   2021; Foster et al., 2021; Agarwal et al., 2023; Zhao et al., 2023) for modeling complex function
131   spaces like neural networks. Among these, Agarwal et al. (2023); Zhao et al. (2023) achieved the
132   best-known regret guarantees under general function approximation by introducing the concept of
133   generalized Eluder dimension to handle weighted regression. Inspired by their work, we estimate the
134   value of items (referred to as item-level Q-value) using general function approximation in this paper.
135

225   3.4   G ENERALIZED F UNCTION A PPROXIMATION FOR ITEM - LEVEL Q- FUNCTION
226
      We estimate the item-level Q-functions (referred to as Q-values) using general function approximation.
227
      Specifically, the agent is given a function class F :“ tFh uHh“1 , where each set Fh is composed of
228
      functions fh : S ˆ I Ñ r0, Ls. We assume L “ Op1q throughout the paper. Since no reward is
229   collected in the pH ` 1qth steps, we set fH`1 “ 0. We denote N as the maximal size of function
230   class, i.e., N “ maxhPrHs |Fh |. We consider a class of episodic MDPs such that the value functions
231   satisfy the completeness and realizability assumptions under a function class F.
232   Assumption 2 (Completeness & Realizability). For each h P rHs and any V : S Ñ r0, 1s, we
233                   ‹
      assume that Qh P Fh , and there exists fh , fh1 P Fh such that

236   Remark 1. The completeness and realizability assumptions are standard in RL with general function
237   approximation (Wang et al., 2021; Jin et al., 2021; Agarwal et al., 2023; Zhao et al., 2023). Our
238   assumption is the same as those in Agarwal et al. (2023); Zhao et al. (2023), but stronger than those
239
      in (Wang et al., 2021; Jin et al., 2021), especially, in terms of the second moment completeness.
      However, this assumption is essential for using point-wise exploration bonuses and achieving a
240
      tighter regret bound. Additionally, it naturally holds for both tabular and linear MDPs.

311   4     A LGORITHM
312
313   In this section, we introduce an algorithm, which, to the best of our knowledge, is the first to provide
314   statistical guarantees in combinatorial RL with preference feedback while maintaining computational
315   tractability. Step 1 involves online parameter estimation for the MNL preference model, proposed
316   by (Zhang & Sugiyama, 2024; Lee & Oh, 2024). Steps 2, 3, and 4 implement variance-weighted
317
      regression to tighten the regret bound, as outlined in (Agarwal et al., 2023). Step 5, which is our
      main contribution, ensures optimism in a computationally efficient manner, even with uncertainty in
318
      item-level Q-values. Step 6, inspired by (Agarwal et al., 2023), introduces an exploration step that
319
      employs a modified threshold to account for estimation errors from the MNL preference model.
320
321   Step 1. Loss function for MNL preference model and online parameter estimation (Line 7).
322   At episode k and horizon h, given the user’s choice feedback ckh P Akh , the response for each
323   item aim P Akh is defined as yhk paim q :“ 1pckh “ aim q P t0, 1u. Therefore, the response variable
      yhk :“ pyhk pa0 q, yhk pai1 q, . . . yhk pail qq, where l ď M ´ 1, is sampled from a multinomial distribution:

406   To introduce optimism and encourage exploration, we need to solve the following optimization

408   1, ˘2:
                              ÿ

412
      ř of naive
            k
                  approach to solving the optimization problem in Equation 6 is to add bonus terms to
                  k       k   k     k
       aPA Ph pa|sh , A; θh qfh,j psh , aq for each assortment A, and then enumerating all A P A to find the
413
      maximum. However, this approach results in an exponential computational cost of Op|I|M q.
414
415   To avoid this exponential computational cost, inspired by Oh &       ř Iyengar (2019; 2021),    we use
416   optimistic MNL utilities instead of directly adding bonus terms to aPA Phk pa|skh , A; θhk qfh,j
                                                                                                   k
                                                                                                       pskh , aq.
417   However, unlike MNL bandits (Rusmevichientong et al., 2010; Sauré & Zeevi, 2013; Agrawal et al.,
418
      2017; 2019; Oh & Iyengar, 2019; 2021), simply using optimistic utilities does not always guarantee
      optimism. Specifically, increasing the MNL utilities to their optimistic values (e.g., UCB) reduces
419
      the probability of choosing the outside option a0 , by definition. In MNL bandits, the item values are
420
      known, and the value of the outside option a0 is fixed at zero. Therefore, increasing the MNL utilities
421   (using the optimistic utilities) lowers the probability of choosing the outside option, which in turn
422   increases the expected value of the item values.
423                                                                                                       k
424
      However, in our setting, using the optimistic utilities can decrease the expected value of fh,j        . To
                                                                       ‹
425   explain why: even if the true value of the outside option, Qh ps, a0 q, is the lowest, its estimated value,
        k                                      k               k
426   fh,j ps, a0 q, can be the highest—i.e., fh,j ps, a0 q ą fh,j ps, aq for all a P Izta0 u—due to uncertainty.
                                                                                                        k
427   In this case, increasing the MNL utilities results in a decrease in the expected value of fh,j       . This
                                                             ‹
428   challenge arises from the unknown item values, Qh , which is one of the main difficulties we face in
429   combinatorial RL with preference feedback.
430                                                   k                              ‹
431
      Hence, since we must rely on the estimates fh,j    instead of the true values Qh , a more refined approach
                                               k
      is required to use the utility based on fh,j . Given the confidence interval in Equation 3, we define the

469   Theorem 1 (Regret upper bound of MNL-VQL, proof in Section D ). Suppose Assumptions 1 and 2
470   hold. We assume that we have the generalized Eluder dimension dimν,K pFh q, for h P rHs, as defined
471   in Definition 3 with ρ “ 1, and access to a consistent bonusa          oracle B satisfying Definition   `? 4 with
                                     1
                                        řH
472   ϵb “ Op1{KHq. Let dν “ H                 dim   ν,K pFh q with  ν  “      1{KH,    and   set  u k  “   O     log N ¨
                        ?               ? h“1           ? ˘
473   plog N Nb ¨ H 5{2 dν ` dH 5{2 log N Nb q{ K . Then, for any δ ă 1{pH 2 ` 14q, with probability
474   at least 1 ´ δ, the regret of MNL-VQL is upper-bounded by:
475                              ˆ                                                                                  ˙
                                       ?        1           a
476        RegretpM, Kq “ Õ dH K ` d2 H 2 ` dν HK log N ` dν H 5 log N log2 pN Nb q ,
477                                             κ
                                   loooooooooomoooooooooon  loooooooooooooooooooooooooomoooooooooooooooooooooooooon
478                                    regret from MNL model            regret from general function approximation of Q
479
      where d is the feature dimension of the MNL preference model, N is the maximum size of the function
480
      class, i.e., N “ maxhPrHs |Fh |, and Nb it the size of the bonus function class, i.e., Nb “ |W|.
481
482   Discussion of Theorem 1. The first two terms arise from the regret of the MNL preference model,
483   while the other two terms come from the regret associated with the general function approximation
484   for item-level Q-values. When H “ 1, reducing our setting to MNL bandits (though not exactly
485   the traditional MNL bandits, as we consider a more general case where item values are unknown
      and the value of the outside option can be non-zero), the first two terms of our regret simplify to


                                                                 9
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


486       ?
      Õpd K ` κ1 d2 q. This matches the known minimax optimal regret established by Lee & Oh (2024).
487   Note that we avoid the detrimental dependence on κ in our leading term. The other two terms of
488   our regret, incurred from estimating item-level Q-values using general function approximation, are
489   identical to the regret proposed by Agarwal et al. (2023) are only slightly worse in the lower-order
490   terms compared to Zhao et al. (2023). Since we haven’t focused on optimizing the lower-order terms,
491   we believe they could be easily improved with a more careful analysis, as done by Zhao et al. (2023).
492
      With respect to computational cost, by using the online sensitivity sub-sampling method (Algo-
493   rithm B.1), `we can efficiently implement the bonus oracle      B (see Remark 3), with log |W| “
494                                                             ˘
      log Nb “ Õ maxhPrHs dimν,K pFh q ¨ log N log |S ˆ I| . Furthermore, we can avoid the exponen-
495   tial computational cost required to solve the optimization in Equation 9 (see Remark 4). As a result,
496   our algorithm is both computationally tractable and statistically efficient.
497
498   5.2   L INEAR F UNCTION A PPROXIMATION FOR ITEM - LEVEL Q- FUNCTION
499
500   As a special case, we also consider linear MDPs (Definition 2) with preference feedback. To clearly
501   indicate the dependency on parameters, we denote the linear MDPs as Mθ‹ ,µ‹ ,w‹ , where θ ‹ “
502   tθh‹ uH      ‹    ‹ H            ‹    ‹ H
            h“1 , µ “tµh uh“1 , and w “twh uh“1 . Note that the bonus oracle can be easily implemented
503   using the standard elliptical bonus, which satisfies all the necessary properties (refer Appendix E).
504   Theorem 2 (Informal, Regret upper bound for linear MDPs, complete version in Section E). In
505   linear MDPs, under the same conditions as Theorem 1, with appropriately chosen parameters, the
506   cumulative regret of MNL-VQL is upper-bounded with probability at least 1 ´ δ as:
                                               ˆ                                                     ˙
507                                                  ?        1 2 2       lin
                                                                              ?            lin 6 5
                  Regret pMθ ,µ ,w , Kq “ Õ dH K ` d H ` d
                              ‹  ‹   ‹                                          HK ` pd q H .
508                                                           κ
                                                 loooooooooomoooooooooon loooooooooooomoooooooooooon
509                                               regret from MNL model   regret from linear MDPs
510
511   We also establish a regret lower bound for linear MDPs by constructing a novel multi-layered MDP
512   (see Figure E.1) with a combinatorial action space and preference feedback.
513   Theorem 3 (Regret lower bound for linear MDPs, proof in Section F). Suppose that d ě 2, dlin ě 6,
514   H ě 3, and K ě maxtC ¨ pdlin ´ 5q2 HpH ` 1q2 , C 1 ¨ pd ´ 1q4 p1 ` Hq{Hu for some constant
515   C, C 1 ą 0. Then, for any algorithm, there exists an episodic linear MDP Mθ,µ,w with MNL
516
      preference feedback such that the worst-case expected regret is lower bounded as follows:
                                                                ´ ?            ?     ¯
517                    sup Eθ,µ,w rRegret pMθ,µ,w , Kqs “ Ω d HK ` dlin HK .
518                   θ,µ,w

519                                                    `                     ˘

      Discussion of Theorem 2 and 3. If K ě Õ d2 H 2 {κ2 `pdlin q10 H 9 , the regret upper bound for
                                      ?        lin
                                                   ?
521   linear MDPs scales as ?ÕpdH K ` d             HKq. Comparing this to the lower bound, there is only
522   a gap of a factor of ?H in the regret term from the MNL model (the first term in each regret).
523   Therefore, if dlin ?
                         ě d H, our algorithm achieves the minimax optimal regret (up to logarithmic
524   factors) of Õpdlin HKq. Note that if we rescale the rewards to be 1{H in the lower bounds of Zhou
525   et al. (2021a) to align with
                               ? our setting, their regret bound becomes the same as the second term of
526   our regret bound, Ωpdlin HKq, which is related to linear function approximation. To the best of our
527   knowledge, this is the first regret lower bound in linear MDPs with preference feedback.
528   Numerical Experiment. We empirically evaluate the performance of our algorithm in linear
529   MDPs (with preference feedback), showing that it outperforms baseline algorithms while remaining
530   computationally efficient even with a large number of items |I|. Due to space constraints, the results
531   are provided in Appendix G.
532
533   6     C ONCLUSION
534
535    In this work, we study combinatorial RL with preference feedback, extending MNL bandit problems
536   to account for the influence of user states and state transitions in applications like recommendation
537   systems. Assuming an MNL preference model with linear utilities and using general function
538   approximation for item values, we propose an efficient algorithm, MNL-VQL, which, to the best of
539   our knowledge, provides the first statistical guarantee. As a special case, in linear MDPs, MNL-VQL
      achieves near-optimal regret, compared to our established lower bound.

      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025

      We provide all the assumptions necessary to derive our theoretical results in Section 3, and the
543   complete proofs of our main results and claims are included in the Appendix.

806                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                              Figure A.1: Illustration of combinatorial RL with preference feedback.
807
808   In this section, we provide additional explanation of our framework, combinatorial RL with preference
809   feedback, for better clarity. In this framework, at each episode, as a user arrives at the system (starting
      in the initial state, e.g., high loyalty), a learning agent selects an assortment A (a set of items) and


                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                     15
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


810
      offers it to the user (the first figure in Figure A.1). The user then chooses an item from the assortment
811   A (the second figure in Figure A.1). The agent receives a reward, along with preference (or choice)
812   feedback, and transitions to the next state (e.g., lower loyalty) (the last figure in Figure A.1). This
813   process repeats until the episode concludes.
814
      The key advantage of this framework is its ability to capture the long-term value of choosing an item
815
      by considering state transitions and avoiding myopic decisions. For instance, in Figure A.1, a user
816
      may choose a junk item that provides a high immediate reward. However, repeatedly recommending
817   such items can lead to user fatigue, resulting in a transition to a state of lower satisfaction or loyalty
818   to the system, ultimately leading to a lower cumulative reward.
819
820
      We compare our framework with other related works.
821   vs MNL bandits. Our framework can be considered as a multi-step extension of MNL bandits (Rus-
822   mevichientong et al., 2010; Sauré & Zeevi, 2013; Agrawal et al., 2017; 2019; Oh & Iyengar, 2019;
823   2021; Perivier & Goyal, 2022; Agrawal et al., 2023; Zhang & Sugiyama, 2024; Lee & Oh, 2024). In
824   MNL bandits, there are no state transitions; thus, in Figure E.1, the user exits the system immediately
825   after receiving a reward.
826   Another important difference is that, in MNL bandits, the value (reward) of choosing an item is
827   assumed to be known, and the value of choosing the outside option a0 is always assumed to be zero.
828   In contrast, in our framework, the value of choosing an item is unknown due to the stochastic nature
829   of rewards and transition probabilities. Additionally, we allow the value of choosing the outside
830   option a0 to be non-zero.
831   vs Cascading RL. In cascading RL (Du et al., 2024), the agent also selects a set of items, and state
832   transitions are taken into account when making decisions. However, these items are offered to the
833   user one by one, and the user decides whether to choose the currently offered item.
834
      For example, in Figure E.1, only the first item, item 2, is shown to the user. If the user chooses item
835
      2, the agent receives a reward and transitions to the next state. If the user does not choose item 2, the
836   next item, item 4, is shown. If the user does not choose any item, the agent considers this as choosing
837   the outside option a0 and transitions to the next state.
838
839
      Cascading RL fundamentally differs from our framework because the user does not compare multiple
      items at once, so it does not involve relative preference feedback. Another key distinction is that in
840
      cascading RL, the probability of choosing each item is independent of the others in the chosen set of
841
      items In contrast, in our MNL preference model, the choice probability of an item is influenced by
842   the other items in the assortment.
843
844   vs PbRL. In preference-based RL (PbRL) (Akrour et al., 2012; Wirth et al., 2017; Christiano et al.,
845
      2017; Ouyang et al., 2022; Saha et al., 2023; Zhu et al., 2023; Zhan et al., 2023), the agent learns not
      from explicit numerical rewards, but through preferences as feedback. The user is presented with two
846
      (or sometimes multiple) items and chooses a preferred one.
847
848   In our framework, if we treat the reward signal generated by the user’s choice as a preference
849   signal instead of a numerical reward, we can learn the policy based on user preferences, similar to
850   PbRL, without relying on explicit rewards. However, our framework differs fundamentally from
851
      PbRL because our goal is not just to offer a single item, but multiple items—a combinatorial (base)
      action—at each timestep.
852
853
854
855

      et al. (2023), which builds on the original sensitivity sub-sampling method proposed by Kong et al.
861
      (2021) and Wang et al. (2020).

       Design of exploration policy. At each episode k, the agent collects data using both Akh,1 and

       Akh,2 , where Akh,j P arg maxAPA aPA P           rk pa|sk , Aqf k psk , aq for j “ 1, 2. Given a sequence of

                   #

1232   We denote hk P rH ` 1s as the (random) horizon at which the agent first begins offering the
1233   assortment Akh,2 . More formally, for h ď hk , the assortment offered is Akh “ Akh,1 , and for h ě hk ,
1234   the assortment offered is Akh “ Akh,2 . We then divide the set of episodes rKs into two disjoint subsets:
1235   Ko and Koo , such that
1236                  Ko :“ tk P rKs : hk “ H ` 1u, and Koo :“ tk P rKs : hk ď Hu.

       D.2    C ONFIDENCE I NTERVALS AND GOOD EVENTS

1244   In this subsection, we show that, given the construction of confidence intervals Chk and Fh,j
                                                                                                  k
                                                                                                     for
1245                               θ
       j “ 1, ˘2, the good events E and EďK occurs with high probability.
1246
       Proposition D.1 (Online parameter confidence interval, Lemma 1 of Lee & Oh 2024). Let δ P p0, 1q.
1247
       Under Assumption 1, for the confidence interval defined in Equation 3 with
1248                  « ˜                                   ˆ                 ˆ ?         ˙
1249              k                                            17      ?        2 1 ` 2k
                αh “ 2η p3 logp1 ` pM ` 1qkq ` B ` 2q             λ ` 2 λ log
1250                                                           16                   δ
1251                                                 ?
                         ˆ ˆ ?            ˙˙2 ˙                  ˆ          ˙     ¸         ff1{2
1252                            2 1 ` 2k            7 6                k`1                2
                    ` 16 log                      `      dη log 1 `           ` 2 ` λB
1253                                δ                 6                 2λ
1254                     ?
1255                “ Op d log k log M q,
1256
                                              ?
       η “ 12 logpM ` 1q ` B ` 1 and λ “ 84 2dη, and for any h P rHs, we have
1257
1258                                       Prr@k ě 1, θh‹ P Chk s ě 1 ´ δ.
1259
1260   Now, we define the good event for the preference model E θ as follows:
1261

       Then, by applying proposition D.1 and using a union bound over h P rHs, we obtain the following
       corollary:

       in Proposition D.1, for δ P p0, 1q, with probability at least 1 ´ δ, the good event for the preference
       model E θ happens, i.e., θh‹ P Chk for all k ě 1 and all h P rHs.

       In this section, we introduce several properties of linear function class. For linear MDPs, let Fhlin pϵc q

       A good policy is one that quickly reaches the state xH`2 while remaining in the lower layers (i.e.,
2942   with lower i). Recall that the item a‹h has the highest utility and, therefore, the highest choice
2943                                                                              piq
       probability. It also has the best chance of quickly reaching the state xH`2 while staying within
2944   the same layer. In other words, a good policy encourages the user to frequently select the item
2945   a‹h P arg maxaPIzta0 u xµh , ay “ arg maxaPIzta0 u xθh‹ , ϕps, aqy. Note that a‹h is unique due to the
2946   way the action space and transition probabilities are constructed.

3334   In this section, we empirically evaluate the performance of our algorithm, MNL-VQL, in linear MDPs.
3335   We consider an online shopping with budget (refer Figure G.1) environment under linear MDPs and
3336   an MNL user preference model. We denote the set of states as S “ ts1 , . . . , s|S| u and the set of items
3337   as I “ ta1 , . . . , aN , a0 u (a0 denotes the outside option). Each state sj P S corresponds to a user’s
3338   budget level, where a larger index j indicates a higher budget (e.g., s|S| represents the state with the
3339   largest budget). The initial state is set to the medium budget state sr|S|{2s . Furthermore, we let the
3340   transition probabilities Ph , rewards rh , and preference model Ph be the same for all h P rHs, and
3341   thus we omit the subscript h.
3342   At state sj , the agent offers an assortment A P A with a maximum size of M . The user then either
3343   purchases an item ai P A or or opts not to buy anything, represented by the outside option a0 P A.
3344   Then, the reward is defined as follows:
3345                                                                              ´           ¯
                                                                                     i     j
3346          • If the user purchases an item ai P A, the reward is: rpsj , ai q “ 100N ` |S|   {H.
3347
               • If the user does not buy anything (a0 ), the reward is: rpsj , a0 q “ 0.

3368   The reward can be regarded as the user’s rating of the purchased item. It is reasonable to assume
3369   that, at higher budget states, users tend to be more generous in their ratings, leading to higher ratings
3370   (rewards). And the transition probability is defined as follows:
3371
3372          • If the user purchases an item ai P A, the transition probability is:

                                    Ppsminpj`1,|S|q |sj , ai q “ 1 ´           ,         and Ppsmaxpj´1,0q |sj , ai q “                .

              • If the user does not buy anything (a0 ), the transition probability is:

3378
       If the user does purchase an item, the budget level decreases with a certain probability that depends
3379
       on the chosen item. Conversely, if the user does not purchase any item (a0 ), the budget level increases
3380
       deterministically.
3381
3382   We construct the feature map ψps, aq (for linear MDPs) using SVD. Specifically, the transition kernel
3383   Pp¨|¨, ¨q P R|S||I|ˆ|S| has at most |S| singular values, and the reward vector rp¨, ¨q P R|S||I| has one
3384   singular value. Consequently, the feature map ψps, aq P Rdlin lies in a space of dimension |S| ` 1,
3385   i.e., dlin “ |S| ` 1.
3386   For MNL preference model, the true parameter θ ‹ P Rd , and the feature ϕps, aq P Rd (for MNL
3387   preference model) are randomly sampled from a d-dimensional uniform distribution in each instance.
3388
       We set K “ 30000, H “ 5, M “ 6, |S| “ 5, d “ 5 (feature dimension for MNL preference
3389
       model), dlin “ 6 (feature dimension for linear MDP), N P t10, 20, 40u (the number of items), and
3390          řM ´1 `N ˘
       |A| “ m1 “1 m      P t637, 21699, 760098u (the number of assortments). Moreover, for simplicity,
3391            k
3392
       we set σ̄h “ 1 in our algorithm. As a result, we use unweighted regression to estimate the Q-values.
3393   We compare our algorithm with two baselines: Myopic and LSVI-UCB (Jin et al., 2020). Myopic is a
3394   variant of OFU-MNL+ (Lee & Oh, 2024) adapted for unknown rewards. It is a myopic algorithm that
3395   selects assortments based only on immediate rewards, ignoring state transitions. LSVI-UCB (Jin et al.,
3396   2020) treats each assortment as a single, atomic (holistic) action, requiring enumeration of all possible
3397   assortments. To demonstrate the effectiveness of our approach, we also include the performance of
3398
       the optimal policy (Optimal) to highlight that our algorithm is converging toward optimality. We run
       the algorithms on 10 independent instances and report the episodic return across all episodes.
3399
3400   Figure G.2 demonstrates that our algorithm significantly outperforms other baseline algorithms.
3401   And Table G.1 shows that our algorithm remains robust even as the total number of assortments
       |A| increases. Although the runtime of Myopic is approximately 5.3 times faster than ours, its


                                                                           63
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


3402
       performance is substantially worse, converging to a suboptimal solution. This underscores a key
3403   limitation of the myopic strategy—it can completely fail in certain environments, highlighting the
3404   importance of accounting for long-term outcomes. Additionally, the runtime of LSVI-UCB increases
3405   exponentially as N grows, because it requires enumerating all possible assortments. Due to the
3406   extremely slow runtime of LSVI-UCB, we did not include its performance results for N “ 20 and
3407   N “ 40. Instead, for these cases, we used dotted lines to represent the average episodic return
3408   observed for N “ 10. Even for the smaller case of N “ 10, LSVI-UCB demonstrated the worst
3409   performance. Based on this observation, we suspect that its performance is unlikely to improve as N
3410   increases.
